		<div  data-elementor-type="single" data-elementor-id="1899" class="elementor elementor-1899 elementor-location-single post-23217 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-attack-trends blog_author-igal-lytzki" data-elementor-post-type="elementor_library">
			<div  class="elementor-element elementor-element-435e2df e-flex e-con-boxed e-con e-parent" data-id="435e2df" data-element_type="container">
					<div  class="e-con-inner">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-23af678 elementor-widget elementor-widget-breadcrumbs" data-id="23af678" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="breadcrumbs.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<p id="breadcrumbs"><span><span><a href="https://perception-point.io/">Perception Point</a></span> » <span><a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/">Blog</a></span> » <span><a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/category/attack-trends/">Attack Trends</a></span> » <span class="breadcrumb_last" aria-current="page"><strong>Behind the Attack: Remcos RAT</strong></span></span></p>				</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-c4c7848 elementor-widget elementor-widget-heading" data-id="c4c7848" data-element_type="widget" data-settings="{&quot;_animation&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;_animation_mobile&quot;:&quot;none&quot;}" data-widget_type="heading.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<p class="elementor-heading-title elementor-size-default">August 21, 2022</p>				</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-32b01ba elementor-widget elementor-widget-heading" data-id="32b01ba" data-element_type="widget" data-settings="{&quot;_animation&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;_animation_delay&quot;:200,&quot;_animation_mobile&quot;:&quot;none&quot;}" data-widget_type="heading.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<h1 class="elementor-heading-title elementor-size-default">Behind the Attack: Remcos RAT</h1>				</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-554c7d4 elementor-widget__width-initial elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="554c7d4" data-element_type="widget" data-settings="{&quot;_animation&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;_animation_delay&quot;:400,&quot;_animation_mobile&quot;:&quot;none&quot;}" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
									In this blog post, we break down the attack chain of a recent Remcos RAT malware campaign.								</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-072e1f1 elementor-widget elementor-widget-post-info" data-id="072e1f1" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="post-info.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
							<ul class="elementor-inline-items elementor-icon-list-items elementor-post-info">
								<li class="elementor-icon-list-item elementor-repeater-item-844ed94 elementor-inline-item">
													<span class="elementor-icon-list-text elementor-post-info__item elementor-post-info__item--type-custom">
										Igal Lytzki, IR Team					</span>
								</li>
				</ul>
						</div>
				</div>
					</div>
				</div>
		<div  class="elementor-element elementor-element-c646816 e-flex e-con-boxed e-con e-parent" data-id="c646816" data-element_type="container">
					<div  class="e-con-inner">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-b27becd e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="b27becd" data-element_type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-e7094d2 post-content elementor-widget__width-initial elementor-widget-tablet__width-initial elementor-widget-mobile__width-inherit elementor-widget elementor-widget-theme-post-content" data-id="e7094d2" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="theme-post-content.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Remcos RAT is a </span><a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/malware/how-prevent-malware-attacks/"><span style="font-weight: 400;">malware</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;"> classified as a </span><a href="https://www.techtarget.com/searchsecurity/definition/RAT-remote-access-Trojan"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Remote Access Trojan</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;">. This means that it is usually used by threat actors to control infected PCs remotely or spy on users by capturing keystrokes, watching them through their webcam, or even listening to their microphone.&nbsp;</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Through Remcos RAT, threat actors can remotely execute malicious tasks on a user’s computer and steal any sensitive data that is available to them.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Read on to learn about Remcos RAT’s attack chain and the deeper implications of this malware’s capabilities.</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">The Phishing Email</h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Like most attacks these days, Remcos RAT’s payload is delivered via </span><a href="https://perception-point.io/channel-coverage/email-security/"><span style="font-weight: 400;">email</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;">, the number one attack vector for cyberattacks.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The user receives an email, posing as an urgent invoice request. In the text of the message, the sender urges the recipient to open the attached invoice, which is actually an embedded URL in a picture. Once the user clicks on the attachment, they are redirected to a OneDrive automated download which triggers the initial payload.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">There are several warning signs within the email to indicate that it may be malicious: 1) the subject line is generic, 2) the sender originates from a Gmail address, indicating it is not affiliated with an official company, 3) the message itself is concise with the clear goal of prompting the reader to click on the attachments.</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img fetchpriority="high" decoding="async" width="421" height="293" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20421%20293'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT email" class="wp-image-23236" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture1-8.png.webp 421w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture1-8-300x209.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 421px) 100vw, 421px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture1-8.png.webp" /><noscript><img fetchpriority="high" decoding="async" width="421" height="293" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture1-8.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT email" class="wp-image-23236" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture1-8.png.webp 421w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture1-8-300x209.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 421px) 100vw, 421px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 1: The email with the fake invoice attachments, requesting an invoice (Slovenian).</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><b>The Remcos RAT Attack Flow</b></h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Now, let’s review the components of the attack, breaking it down step by step. Below, you can see a visual map of the steps involved in the attack:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="421" height="406" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20421%20406'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT attack flow" class="wp-image-23235" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture2-1.png.webp 421w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture2-1-300x289.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 421px) 100vw, 421px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture2-1.png.webp" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="421" height="406" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture2-1.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT attack flow" class="wp-image-23235" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture2-1.png.webp 421w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture2-1-300x289.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 421px) 100vw, 421px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 2: The attack flow</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 1: Once the user clicks on the OneDrive URL, an ace type archive is automatically downloaded; inside of it we can find Faktura 9382022.vbs.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 2: While Faktura 9382022.vbs is being executed, it downloads an additional payload located on one of the threat actor servers (GH2.jpg).</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 3: GH2.jpg (a JavaScript file) is then executed, containing inside of it and evoking an embedded </span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PowerShell"><span style="font-weight: 400;">PowerShell script</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;">.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 4: The PowerShell script creates an alias for IEX (set to the char: “P”) and downloads an additional payload located on the same server from Stage 2 (GH1.jpg).</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 5: GH1.jpg (a PowerShell file) contains two embedded files: Sneaky.dll and archived Remcos. During the execution process of the script, the dll is invoked.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 6: Next, Sneaky.dll loads the archived Remcos malware, unpacks it from the archive, and injects it into RegAsm.exe (a legitimate Microsoft .NET executable).</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Stage 7: The injected Remcos RAT connects with the threat actor’s C2 server and waits for C&amp;C instructions.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Keep reading for a detailed analysis of the files and commands used throughout the attack.</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><b>Initial Access</b></h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The initial VBScript, “Faktura 9382022.vbs”, is heavily obfuscated, containing several replacement functions and some “clean bytes” in order to pass static analysis engines (such as VirusTotal):</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="282" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20282'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23234" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture3-3.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture3-3-300x136.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture3-3.png.webp" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="624" height="282" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture3-3.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23234" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture3-3.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture3-3-300x136.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 3: Faktura 9382022.vbs</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">After cleaning the script we can see that the script has two main functions. The first is copying itself to the startup folder of the user’s computer (in order to maintain persistence):&nbsp;</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="164" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20164'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23233" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture4.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture4-300x79.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture4.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="164" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture4.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23233" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture4.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture4-300x79.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 4: Function 1</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"><br>
</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"><br>
</span><span style="font-weight: 400;">The second thing that the script does is create a MSXML document. By using the load method within, the script executes an additional payload located on a remote server (hxxps[://]solutionias[.]com/bin/GH2[.]jpg):</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="106" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20106'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23232" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture5.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture5-300x51.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture5.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="106" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture5.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23232" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture5.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture5-300x51.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;"></span> <span style="font-weight: 400;"><br>
</span><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 5: Function 2&nbsp;</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><b>Unwrapping the Obfuscations</b></h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">If we manually navigate to the threat actor’s server, we can see that this is not a picture&nbsp; but&nbsp; rather an xsl file containing JavaScript script:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="138" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20138'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23231" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture6.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture6-300x66.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture6.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="138" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture6.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23231" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture6.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture6-300x66.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 6: JavaScript script</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Within the script, you can see a large string passed to a certain function. When you look at the string itself, you can see that the script has a </span><a href="https://dencode.com/en/string/hex#:~:text=Hex%20string%20is%20the%20binary,Bin%20string"><span style="font-weight: 400;">HEX-type string</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;"> structure:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="147" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20147'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23230" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture7.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture7-300x71.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture7.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="147" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture7.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23230" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture7.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture7-300x71.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 7: HEX string</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"><br>
</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">We input this string to </span><a href="https://gchq.github.io/CyberChef/"><span style="font-weight: 400;">CyberChef</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;"> and applied the “From HEX” recipe to give us a brand new obfuscated PowerShell command:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="381" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20381'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23229" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture8.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture8-300x183.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture8.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="381" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture8.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23229" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture8.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture8-300x183.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 8: The new PowerShell command</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">When analyzing the PowerShell script further, the threat actor does something unique: he creates a new alias for the IEX (Invoke-Expression) command. By doing this he can call the IEX command without writing down IEX, which can help the attack bypass static AV detection:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="149" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20149'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23228" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture9.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture9-300x72.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture9.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="149" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture9.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23228" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture9.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture9-300x72.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 9: The new alias for the IEX command</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"><br>
</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"><br>
</span><span style="font-weight: 400;">As we’ve already seen, this PowerShell script also contains a variable with a binary-type string. When you take this string to CyberChef and apply “From Binary” (delimiter: comma, Byte Length: 8), the output gives us a new obfuscated PowerShell script:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="260" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20260'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23227" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture10.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture10-300x125.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture10.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="260" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture10.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23227" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture10.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture10-300x125.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 10: the new obfuscated PowerShell script</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Once we cleaned up the script, we noticed three main things occurring:</span></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">The script sets up the security protocol to be TLS 1.2.</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">It alerts Google to ensure that the protocol configuration works.</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">It creates a WebClient object to download and invoke the additional payload located on the same remote server as we previously observed (hxxps[://]solutionias[.]com/bin/GH1[.]jpg).</span></li>
</ul>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="92" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%2092'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23226" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture11.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture11-300x44.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture11.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="92" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture11.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23226" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture11.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture11-300x44.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 11: The three actions</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><b>DLL Loading &amp; Process Hollowing</b></h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The payload that was downloaded also has a jpg extension. By navigating back again to the threat actor’s remote server, we can see that the threat actor uses the method of trying to hide the real file extension by changing it to a jpg:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="89" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%2089'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23225" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture12.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture12-300x43.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture12.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="89" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture12.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23225" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture12.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture12-300x43.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 12: Hiding the file extension as a jpg</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The downloaded script is actually another PowerShell script that contains two big variables &#8211;&nbsp; $MNB and $fbOd:</span></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">$hoDNhAI – unpacks the $MNB variable using an embedded function (Sneaky.dll)</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">$fbOd – Archived Remcos agent</span></li>
</ul>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="55" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%2055'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23224" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture13.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture13-300x26.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture13.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="55" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture13.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23224" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture13.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture13-300x26.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">There is also the $ayy variable which is responsible for loading the dll (stored on $hoDNhAI):</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"></span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 13: $ayy variable</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">We can see in the figure above that when Sneaky.dll is loaded, the script invokes a method called: “Black”. This method is located under the “toooyou” class in the “Sneaky” namespace:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="218" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20218'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23223" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture14.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture14-300x105.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture14.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="218" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture14.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23223" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture14.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture14-300x105.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 14: “Sneaky” namespace</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Since the dll is heavily obfuscated, we used </span><a href="https://github.com/de4dot/de4dot"><span style="font-weight: 400;">De4Dot</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;"> to deobfuscate it and make the dll a bit more readable. We can see that the method that was invoked receives two arguments:</span></p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">String tt&nbsp;</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Byte[] ukk</span></li>
</ol>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">These arguments are passed in the PowerShell script; the string that is passed is “RegAsm.exe”; the byte array is the archived Remcos.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Following the flow of the “Black” method, we can see that a new array object is declared, containing the string that was passed. The result of the method is “GLPFPGLLRRR”, which receives the archived Remcos as argument):</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="451" height="136" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20451%20136'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23222" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture15.png.webp 451w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture15-300x90.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 451px) 100vw, 451px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture15.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="451" height="136" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture15.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23222" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture15.png.webp 451w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture15-300x90.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 451px) 100vw, 451px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 15: GLPFGLLRRRR</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">This function is responsible for unpacking the Remcos agent out of the gzip archive. After that, the dll starts the hollowing process by spawning the legitimate RegAsm.exe process in suspended mode and inject the unpacked Remcos agent:</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="292" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20292'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23221" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture16.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture16-300x140.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture16.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="292" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture16.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23221" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture16.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture16-300x140.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 16: Unpacking the Remcos agent</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="65" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%2065'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23220" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture17.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture17-300x31.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture17.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="65" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture17.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23220" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture17.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture17-300x31.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 17: The hollowing process</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Finally, the RegAsm.exe process runs on the user’s system with the injected Remcos RAT inside of it. The RAT will begin to harvest information, creating mutex and persistence files.</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><b>Extracting Remcos Configurations</b></h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Once we unpacked the Remcos agent, we extracted the configurations set by the author completely statically by following the next steps:</span></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Open up the Remcos agent in CFFExplorer.</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Navigate to Resource Editor -&gt; RCData -&gt; SETTINGS.&nbsp;</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">The first byte of the resource indicates the key length (the data is encrypted with RC4 encryption and marked in blue in the picture below).</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">What follows are the next key_length bytes, which is the actual RC4 key (marked in orange).</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">The rest of the remaining bytes are the encrypted data (marked in red).</span></li>
</ul>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="411" height="391" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20411%20391'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23219" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture18.png.webp 411w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture18-300x285.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 411px) 100vw, 411px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture18.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="411" height="391" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture18.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23219" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture18.png.webp 411w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture18-300x285.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 411px) 100vw, 411px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 18: Remcos configurations</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">If you enter the key and the data to CyberChef, you can easily decrypt the data and see the configurations of this Remcos agent (make sure to put key format to HEX and also to input format to HEX):&nbsp;</span></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter"><img decoding="async" width="624" height="362" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20624%20362'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23218" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture19.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture19-300x174.png.webp 300w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture19.png.webp" /><noscript><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="624" height="362" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture19.png.webp" alt="Remcos RAT" class="wp-image-23218" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture19.png.webp 624w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Picture19-300x174.png.webp 300w" sizes="(max-width: 624px) 100vw, 624px" /></noscript></figure>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Figure 19: Decrypted Remcos configurations</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Extracted Configuration:</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Host:port:password &#8211; menz.ddns.net:9001:1</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Assigned name – AUG</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Copy file – vlc.exe</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Copy folder &#8211; vlc</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Startup value – Remcos</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Mutex – Grace-JFMZCP&nbsp;</span></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><b>Recommendations</b></h2>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Remcos <a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/revenge-rat-back-from-microsoft-excel-macros/">RAT malware</a> can cause major damage to an organization when deployed on a device of a highly valued employee. It is usually delivered via </span><a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/phishing/how-to-prevent-phishing-attacks/"><span style="font-weight: 400;">phishing</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;"> email, targeting the specific person (known as spear phishing or whale phishing). There isn’t much that the user has to do in order to initiate the attack; it takes just one click &#8211; one human error &#8211; and this massive attack chain can be easily executed.</span></p>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">To avoid becoming the next Remcos RAT victim, we recommend taking the following steps to mitigate your risk:</span></p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Educate employees on the need for email security and the risk of opening suspicious emails and attachments.&nbsp;</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Run email security drills every few months to ensure that employees know what to look for in a suspicious email.&nbsp;</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Create a process for employees to follow when they receive a suspicious email or link.</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Do not open files with strange links or attachments.</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Always double check the identity of the sender.</span></li>



<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Deploy an advanced email security solution that prevents these malicious emails from reaching users’ inboxes.</span></li>
</ol>



<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Interested in protecting your organization from malware? Learn more </span><a href="https://perception-point.io/malware-detection/"><span style="font-weight: 400;">here</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400;">.</span></p>

<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">menz.ddns.net</span></p>

<script type="rocketlazyloadscript" data-minify="1" data-rocket-type="text/javascript" data-rocket-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/cache/min/1/i/livevalidation_standalone.compressed.js?ver=1742928057" data-rocket-defer defer></script>
<script type="rocketlazyloadscript">
    function handleFormSubmit(ele) {
        var submitButton = ele.querySelector('input[type=submit]');
        var spinner = document.createElement('span');
        spinner.setAttribute('class', 'loader');
        submitButton.setAttribute('disabled', true);
        submitButton.style.cursor = 'wait';
        submitButton.parentNode.appendChild(spinner);
        return true;
    }

    function resetSubmitButton(e) {
        var submitButtons = e.target.form.getElementsByClassName('submit-button');
        for (var i = 0; i < submitButtons.length; i++) {
            submitButtons[i].disabled = false;
        }
    }

    function addChangeHandler(elements) {
        for (var i = 0; i < elements.length; i++) {
            elements[i].addEventListener('change', resetSubmitButton);
        }
    }

    var form = document.getElementById('form11580');
    addChangeHandler(form.getElementsByTagName('input'));
    addChangeHandler(form.getElementsByTagName('select'));
    addChangeHandler(form.getElementsByTagName('textarea'));
    var nodes = document.querySelectorAll('#form11580 input[data-subscription]');
    if (nodes) {
        for (var i = 0, len = nodes.length; i < len; i++) {
            var status = nodes[i].dataset ? nodes[i].dataset.subscription : nodes[i].getAttribute('data-subscription');
            if (status === 'true') {
                nodes[i].checked = true;
            }
        }
    }
    ;
    var nodes = document.querySelectorAll('#form11580 select[data-value]');
    if (nodes) {
        for (var i = 0; i < nodes.length; i++) {
            var node = nodes[i];
            var selectedValue = node.dataset ? node.dataset.value : node.getAttribute('data-value');
            if (selectedValue) {
                for (var j = 0; j < node.options.length; j++) {
                    if (node.options[j].value === selectedValue) {
                        node.options[j].selected = 'selected';
                        break;
                    }
                }
            }
        }
    }
    this.getParentElement = function (list) {
        return list[list.length - 1].parentElement
    };

<script type="rocketlazyloadscript">
    document.onload = handleDocumentLoad('form11580', '3049749');

    function handleDocumentLoad(b, a) {
        window.getElqFormSubmissionToken(b, a);
        window.processLastFormField()
    }

    function getElqFormSubmissionToken(g, c) {
        var e = new XMLHttpRequest();
        var b = document.getElementById(g);
        if (b && b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken")) {
            var f = b.action;
            var a = window.getHostName(f);
            a = "https://" + a + "/e/formsubmittoken?elqSiteID=" + c;
            if (a) {
                e.onreadystatechange = function () {
                    if (e.readyState === 4) {
                        if (e.status === 200) {
                            b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken").value = e.responseText
                        } else {
                            b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken").value = ""
                        }
                    }
                };
                e.open("GET", a, true);
                e.send()
            } else {
                b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken").value = ""
            }
        }
    }

    function getHostName(b) {
        if (typeof window.URL === "function") {
            return new window.URL(b).hostname
        } else {
            var a = b.match(/:\/\/(www[0-9]?\.)?(.[^\/:]+)/i);
            if (a !== null && a.length > 2 && typeof a[2] === "string" && a[2].length > 0) {
                return a[2]
            } else {
                return null
            }
        }
    }

    function processLastFormField() {
        var form = document.getElementById("form11580");
        var lastFormField = form.querySelector("#elq-FormLastRow");
        lastFormField.style.display = "none";
    }
</script>

				const lazyloadRunObserver = () => {
					const lazyloadBackgrounds = document.querySelectorAll( `.e-con.e-parent:not(.e-lazyloaded)` );
					const lazyloadBackgroundObserver = new IntersectionObserver( ( entries ) => {
						entries.forEach( ( entry ) => {
							if ( entry.isIntersecting ) {
								let lazyloadBackground = entry.target;
								if( lazyloadBackground ) {
									lazyloadBackground.classList.add( 'e-lazyloaded' );
								}
								lazyloadBackgroundObserver.unobserve( entry.target );
							}
						});
					}, { rootMargin: '200px 0px 200px 0px' } );
					lazyloadBackgrounds.forEach( ( lazyloadBackground ) => {
						lazyloadBackgroundObserver.observe( lazyloadBackground );
					} );
				};
				const events = [
					'DOMContentLoaded',
					'elementor/lazyload/observe',
				];
				events.forEach( ( event ) => {
					document.addEventListener( event, lazyloadRunObserver );
				} );
